W(C) =


#### 7.4b Role of Expectation Values and Wilson Loops in String Theory

In string theory, the expectation values of Wilson loops play a crucial role in understanding the dynamics of the theory. They provide a way to calculate the probability of a string interacting with a loop, which is a fundamental aspect of the theory.

The expectation value of the Wilson loop can be calculated using the path integral formalism of quantum mechanics. This involves integrating over all possible paths of the string, weighted by the action of the string. The path integral can be written as:

$$
\langle W(C) \rangle = \int_{\mathcal{C}} DX e^{iS[X]}
$$

where $\mathcal{C}$ is the set of all possible paths of the string, $X$ is the position of the string, and $S[X]$ is the action of the string.

The Wilson loop operator is defined as:

$$
W(C) = \text{Tr} \left( P \exp \left( i \oint_C dx^\mu A_\mu(x) \right) \right)
$$

where $P$ is the path-ordering operator, $A_\mu(x)$ is the gauge field, and the integral is taken over the loop $C$.

The expectation value of the Wilson loop can be calculated using the path integral formalism. This involves integrating over all possible paths of the string, weighted by the action of the string. The path integral can be written as:

$$
\langle W(C) \rangle = \int_{\mathcal{C}} DX e^{iS[X]}
$$

where $\mathcal{C}$ is the set of all possible paths of the string, $X$ is the position of the string, and $S[X]$ is the action of the string.

The expectation value of the Wilson loop provides a way to calculate the probability of a string interacting with a loop. This is a crucial aspect of string theory, as it allows us to understand the behavior of the theory in the presence of loops.

In the context of two-dimensional Yang–Mills theory, the Wilson loop expectation values have been computed for regular loops $\gamma_1, \ldots, \gamma_n$ based at $o$ and any function $\varphi$ on $G^n$. The function $A\mapsto \varphi\bigl(h_{\gamma_1}(A),\ldots, h_{\gamma_n}(A)\bigr)$ is called a Wilson loop variable, of interest mostly when $\varphi$ is a product of traces of the holonomies in representations of the group $G$.

If $M$ is the plane, then

$$
\int \varphi\bigl(h_{\gamma}(A) \bigr)\,d\mu_T(A) =\int_G \varphi(x) Q_{Ta}(x)\,dx
$$

where $Q_t(y)$ is the heat kernel on the group $G$, $a$ is the area enclosed by the loop $\gamma$, and the integration is with respect to unit-mass Haar measure. This formula was proved by Driver and by Gross et al. using the Gaussian measure construction of the Yang–Mills measure on the plane and by defining parallel transport by interpreting the equation of parallel transport as a Stratonovich stochastic differential equation.

If $M$ is the 2-sphere then

$$
\int \varphi\bigl(h_{\gamma}(A) \bigr)\,d\mu_T(A) =\frac{1}{Q_{Tc}(e)} \int_G \varphi(x) Q_{Ta}(x)Q_{Tb}(x^{-1})\,dx
$$

where now $b$ is the area of the region "outside" the loop $\gamma$, and $c$ is the total area of the 2-sphere.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of expectation values and Wilson loops, and explore their implications in string theory.

#### 7.4c Examples and Case Studies

In this section, we will explore some examples and case studies that illustrate the role of expectation values and Wilson loops in string theory. These examples will provide a deeper understanding of the concepts and their applications in real-time issues and anomalies.

##### Example 1: Two-Dimensional Yang–Mills Theory

In the context of two-dimensional Yang–Mills theory, the Wilson loop expectation values have been computed for regular loops $\gamma_1, \ldots, \gamma_n$ based at $o$ and any function $\varphi$ on $G^n$. The function $A\mapsto \varphi\bigl(h_{\gamma_1}(A),\ldots, h_{\gamma_n}(A)\bigr)$ is called a Wilson loop variable, of interest mostly when $\varphi$ is a product of traces of the holonomies in representations of the group $G$.

The Wilson loop variable can be written as:

$$
A\mapsto \varphi\bigl(h_{\gamma_1}(A),\ldots, h_{\gamma_n}(A)\bigr)
$$

where $h_{\gamma_i}(A)$ is the holonomy of the connection $A$ around the loop $\gamma_i$. The function $\varphi$ is a product of traces of the holonomies in representations of the group $G$.

The Wilson loop variable is of particular interest when $\varphi$ is a product of traces of the holonomies in representations of the group $G$. This is because the Wilson loop variable provides a way to calculate the probability of a string interacting with a loop. This is a crucial aspect of string theory, as it allows us to understand the behavior of the theory in the presence of loops.

##### Example 2: Plane and 2-Sphere

If $M$ is the plane, then

$$
\int \varphi\bigl(h_{\gamma}(A) \bigr)\,d\mu_T(A) =\int_G \varphi(x) Q_{Ta}(x)\,dx
$$

where $Q_t(y)$ is the heat kernel on the group $G$, $a$ is the area enclosed by the loop $\gamma$, and the integration is with respect to unit-mass Haar measure. This formula was proved by Driver and by Gross et al. using the Gaussian measure construction of the Yang–Mills measure on the plane and by defining parallel transport by interpreting the equation of parallel transport as a Stratonovich stochastic differential equation.

If $M$ is the 2-sphere then

$$
\int \varphi\bigl(h_{\gamma}(A) \bigr)\,d\mu_T(A) =\frac{1}{Q_{Tc}(e)} \int_G \varphi(x) Q_{Ta}(x)Q_{Tb}(x^{-1})\,dx
$$

where now $b$ is the area of the region "outside" the loop $\gamma$, and $c$ is the total area of the 2-sphere.

These examples illustrate the role of expectation values and Wilson loops in string theory. They provide a way to calculate the probability of a string interacting with a loop, which is a crucial aspect of the theory. The examples also demonstrate the use of the Wilson loop variable and the Wilson loop operator in calculating these probabilities.




#### 7.4c Advanced Topics on Expectation Values and Wilson Loops

In the previous section, we discussed the role of expectation values of Wilson loops in string theory. In this section, we will delve deeper into the advanced topics related to these concepts.

#### 7.4c.1 Wilson Loops in Higher Dimensions

In the context of string theory, Wilson loops are not limited to two dimensions. They can be extended to higher dimensions, where they play a crucial role in understanding the dynamics of the theory. The expectation values of Wilson loops in higher dimensions can be calculated using the path integral formalism, similar to the two-dimensional case.

The Wilson loop operator in higher dimensions is defined as:

$$
W(C) = \text{Tr} \left( P \exp \left( i \oint_C dx^\mu A_\mu(x) \right) \right)
$$

where $P$ is the path-ordering operator, $A_\mu(x)$ is the gauge field, and the integral is taken over the loop $C$ in the higher-dimensional space.

The expectation value of the Wilson loop in higher dimensions can be calculated using the path integral formalism. This involves integrating over all possible paths of the string, weighted by the action of the string. The path integral can be written as:

$$
\langle W(C) \rangle = \int_{\mathcal{C}} DX e^{iS[X]}
$$

where $\mathcal{C}$ is the set of all possible paths of the string, $X$ is the position of the string, and $S[X]$ is the action of the string.

The expectation value of the Wilson loop provides a way to calculate the probability of a string interacting with a loop in higher dimensions. This is a crucial aspect of string theory, as it allows us to understand the behavior of the theory in the presence of loops in higher dimensions.

#### 7.4c.2 Wilson Loops and Anomalies

In the context of string theory, Wilson loops can also be used to study anomalies. Anomalies are phenomena that violate the symmetries of the theory. They can be studied using the expectation values of Wilson loops.

The expectation value of the Wilson loop can be used to calculate the probability of an anomaly occurring. This is done by considering the Wilson loop around the anomaly. The expectation value of the Wilson loop around the anomaly can be calculated using the path integral formalism, similar to the case of a regular loop.

The expectation value of the Wilson loop around the anomaly can provide insights into the nature of the anomaly and its impact on the theory. This is a powerful tool in the study of anomalies in string theory.

In the next section, we will explore the concept of anomalies in more detail and discuss their role in string theory.




### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the real-time issues and anomalies that arise in the study of string theory. We have seen how the concept of time plays a crucial role in this theory, and how it differs from traditional Newtonian mechanics. We have also delved into the concept of anomalies, which are phenomena that cannot be explained by the current understanding of string theory.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of understanding the role of time in string theory. Unlike classical mechanics, where time is seen as an absolute and universal concept, string theory introduces the concept of "string time" which is dependent on the state of the string. This has significant implications for our understanding of causality and the arrow of time.

We have also seen how anomalies, such as the non-renormalizable nature of string theory, pose a challenge to our understanding of the theory. These anomalies require further research and development in order to fully understand their implications and potential solutions.

Overall, this chapter has provided a deeper understanding of the complexities and challenges of string theory. It is clear that there is still much to be explored and discovered in this fascinating field.

## Chapter: String Theory: A Comprehensive Introduction

### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of string theory, a fundamental theory in modern physics that attempts to unify the four fundamental forces of nature - gravity, electromagnetism, weak nuclear force, and strong nuclear force. String theory is a theory of everything, a theory that aims to explain the fundamental building blocks of the universe and their interactions. It is a theory that has been studied extensively for over 30 years and has led to groundbreaking discoveries and advancements in our understanding of the universe.

In this chapter, we will explore the concept of string theory and its applications in various fields, including particle physics, cosmology, and quantum mechanics. We will also discuss the mathematical framework of string theory, including the use of differential equations and calculus to describe the behavior of strings. Additionally, we will touch upon the concept of string duality, a fundamental principle in string theory that has revolutionized our understanding of the universe.

String theory has been a subject of intense research and debate among physicists for decades. It has been a topic of interest due to its potential to provide a unified theory of all fundamental forces and particles, leading to a deeper understanding of the fundamental laws of nature. In this chapter, we will explore the key concepts and principles of string theory, providing a comprehensive introduction to this fascinating field.

We will begin by discussing the history and development of string theory, from its origins in the 1960s to its current state. We will touch upon the ongoing research and developments in this field, as well as the challenges and controversies surrounding string theory. By the end of this chapter, readers will have a comprehensive understanding of string theory and its potential to revolutionize our understanding of the universe.

## Chapter 8: String Theory: A Comprehensive Introduction




### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fundamental concepts of string theory, including the string model, the concept of vibrating strings, and the role of symmetry in string theory. We have also delved into the mathematical formalism of string theory, discussing the equations of motion and the concept of string states. In this chapter, we will build upon this foundation and explore the concept of probes of the bulk.

The bulk of a string theory is the six-dimensional space where the strings live and interact. This space is often referred to as the string world-sheet. The bulk is a fundamental concept in string theory, as it is where the strings interact and where the fundamental laws of the theory are encoded. However, the bulk is not directly observable. Instead, we can probe the bulk by studying the behavior of strings and their interactions.

In this chapter, we will discuss various probes of the bulk, including the use of branes, the study of string scattering, and the use of string dualities. These probes allow us to gain insight into the properties of the bulk and to test the predictions of string theory. We will also discuss the role of these probes in the development of string theory, from its early days as a theory of hadrons to its current status as a theory of quantum gravity.

As we delve into the concept of probes of the bulk, we will continue to use the mathematical formalism of string theory. This will include the use of equations and mathematical expressions, which will be formatted using the TeX and LaTeX style syntax and rendered using the MathJax library. This will allow us to express complex mathematical concepts in a clear and concise manner.

In the following sections, we will explore the various probes of the bulk in detail, starting with the use of branes.




### Section: 8.1 Pointlike Probes of the Bulk

#### 8.1a Definition and Characteristics of Pointlike Probes of the Bulk

Pointlike probes of the bulk are a fundamental tool in string theory, allowing us to probe the bulk space and gain insight into the properties of the theory. These probes are typically represented by point-like objects, such as quantum dots, which are used to interact with the bulk space and measure its properties.

The working principle of pointlike probes of the bulk is based on the concept of electrostatics. The potential at the probe, denoted as $\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is described by a boundary value problem of electrostatics. The boundary $\mathcal{S}$ is given by the surfaces of the sample and the probe, assumed to be connected at infinity. The potential $\Phi_\text{QD}$ can be expressed using the Green's function formalism as a sum over volume and surface integrals, where $\mathcal{V}$ denotes the volume enclosed by $\mathcal{S}$ and $\mathbf{n}'$ is the surface normal.

$$
\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})=\iiint\limits_\mathcal{V} G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') \frac{\rho(\mathbf{r}')}{e}d^3\mathbf{r}'+ \frac{\epsilon_0}{e}\oint\limits_\mathcal{S} \bigg[G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial\Phi(\mathbf{r}')}{\partial \mathbf{n}'}-\Phi(\mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') }{\partial \mathbf{n}'}\bigg]d^2\mathbf{r}'.
$$

In this expression, $\Phi_\text{QD}$ depends on the charge density $\rho$ inside $\mathcal{V}$ and on the potential $\Phi$ on $\mathcal{S}$ weighted by the Green's function $G(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')$. The Green's function is given by

$$
G(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')=\frac{e}{4\pi\epsilon_0|\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{r}'|} + F(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}'),
$$

where $F$ satisfies the Laplace equation.

By specifying $F$ and thus defining the boundary conditions, these equations can be used to obtain the relation between $\Phi_\text{QD}$ and the surface potential $\Phi_\text{s}(\mathbf{r}'), \quad \mathbf{r}' \in \mathcal{S}$ for more specific measurement situations. The combination of a conductive probe and a conductive surface, a situation characterized by Dirichlet boundary conditions, has been described in detail.

Conceptually, the relation between $\Phi_\text{QD}$ and $\Phi_\text{s}$ can be understood as the interaction between the probe and the bulk space. The probe, by interacting with the bulk space, can measure the properties of the space, such as its potential and charge distribution. This interaction allows us to probe the bulk space and gain insight into the properties of the theory.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the properties of pointlike probes of the bulk and explore their applications in string theory.

#### 8.1b Probing the Bulk with Pointlike Probes

The interaction between the pointlike probe and the bulk space can be further explored by considering the concept of a quantum dot (QD). A QD is a nanometer-sized semiconductor particle that can confine electrons and holes, leading to quantum effects. The quantum confinement of these particles results in discrete energy levels, similar to the discrete modes of a string.

The interaction of a QD with the bulk space can be described by the Green's function formalism, as discussed in the previous section. The potential at the QD, denoted as $\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is given by

$$
\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})=\iiint\limits_\mathcal{V} G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') \frac{\rho(\mathbf{r}')}{e}d^3\mathbf{r}'+ \frac{\epsilon_0}{e}\oint\limits_\mathcal{S} \bigg[G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial\Phi(\mathbf{r}')}{\partial \mathbf{n}'}-\Phi(\mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') }{\partial \mathbf{n}'}\bigg]d^2\mathbf{r}'.
$$

In this equation, $\Phi_\text{QD}$ depends on the charge density $\rho$ inside $\mathcal{V}$ and on the potential $\Phi$ on $\mathcal{S}$ weighted by the Green's function $G(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')$. The Green's function is given by

$$
G(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')=\frac{e}{4\pi\epsilon_0|\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{r}'|} + F(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}'),
$$

where $F$ satisfies the Laplace equation.

By specifying $F$ and thus defining the boundary conditions, these equations can be used to obtain the relation between $\Phi_\text{QD}$ and the surface potential $\Phi_\text{s}(\mathbf{r}'), \quad \mathbf{r}' \in \mathcal{S}$ for more specific measurement situations. The combination of a conductive probe and a conductive surface, a situation characterized by Dirichlet boundary conditions, has been described in detail.

The interaction of a QD with the bulk space can be further explored by considering the concept of a quantum dot (QD). A QD is a nanometer-sized semiconductor particle that can confine electrons and holes, leading to quantum effects. The quantum confinement of these particles results in discrete energy levels, similar to the discrete modes of a string.

The interaction of a QD with the bulk space can be described by the Green's function formalism, as discussed in the previous section. The potential at the QD, denoted as $\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is given by

The combination of a conductive probe and a conductive surface, a situation characterized by Dirichlet boundary conditions, has been described in detail.

#### 8.1c Applications of Pointlike Probes of the Bulk

Pointlike probes of the bulk, such as quantum dots (QDs), have found numerous applications in various fields. These applications range from quantum computing to biomedical imaging, and they are made possible by the unique properties of QDs.

##### Quantum Computing

In quantum computing, QDs are used as quantum bits or qubits due to their discrete energy levels. The energy levels of a QD can be manipulated using external electric fields, allowing for the encoding and processing of quantum information. The long coherence times of QDs make them promising candidates for quantum computing applications.

##### Biomedical Imaging

QDs are also used in biomedical imaging due to their size and fluorescent properties. The small size of QDs allows them to penetrate biological tissues, while their fluorescent properties make them ideal for imaging applications. QDs have been used in a variety of imaging techniques, including multiphoton microscopy and photoacoustic imaging.

##### Other Applications

QDs have also found applications in other fields, such as sensing and energy harvesting. Their small size and unique properties make them ideal for use in these applications.

In conclusion, pointlike probes of the bulk, such as QDs, have a wide range of applications due to their unique properties. Their use in quantum computing, biomedical imaging, and other fields is expected to continue to grow as researchers discover new ways to harness their potential.




#### 8.1b Role of Pointlike Probes of the Bulk in String Theory

Pointlike probes of the bulk play a crucial role in string theory, particularly in the study of the bulk-boundary correspondence. This correspondence is a fundamental concept in string theory, which describes the relationship between the properties of the bulk space and the boundary conditions imposed on it.

The bulk-boundary correspondence is a key concept in string theory, as it provides a way to understand the behavior of strings in the bulk space. The bulk space is the space in which the strings propagate, and it is typically a higher-dimensional space than the space we observe in everyday life. The boundary conditions imposed on the bulk space determine the behavior of the strings, and thus the properties of the bulk space.

Pointlike probes of the bulk are used to probe the bulk space and measure its properties. These probes are typically represented by point-like objects, such as quantum dots, which are used to interact with the bulk space and measure its properties. The potential at the probe, denoted as $\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is described by a boundary value problem of electrostatics. The boundary $\mathcal{S}$ is given by the surfaces of the sample and the probe, assumed to be connected at infinity. The potential $\Phi_\text{QD}$ can be expressed using the Green's function formalism as a sum over volume and surface integrals, where $\mathcal{V}$ denotes the volume enclosed by $\mathcal{S}$ and $\mathbf{n}'$ is the surface normal.

$$
\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})=\iiint\limits_\mathcal{V} G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') \frac{\rho(\mathbf{r}')}{e}d^3\mathbf{r}'+ \frac{\epsilon_0}{e}\oint\limits_\mathcal{S} \bigg[G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial\Phi(\mathbf{r}')}{\partial \mathbf{n}'}-\Phi(\mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') }{\partial \mathbf{n}'}\bigg]d^2\mathbf{r}'.
$$

In this expression, $\Phi_\text{QD}$ depends on the charge density $\rho$ inside $\mathcal{V}$ and on the potential $\Phi$ on $\mathcal{S}$ weighted by the Green's function $G(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')$. The Green's function is given by

$$
G(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')=\frac{e}{4\pi\epsilon_0|\mathbf{r}-\mathbf{r}'|} + F(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}'),
$$

where $F$ satisfies the Laplace equation. By specifying $F$ and thus defining the boundary conditions, these equations can be used to obtain the relation between $\Phi_\text{QD}$ and the surface potential $\Phi_\text{s}(\mathbf{r}'), \quad \mathbf{r}' \in \mathcal{S}$.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of pointlike probes of the bulk and explore their applications in string theory.

#### 8.1c Applications of Pointlike Probes of the Bulk

Pointlike probes of the bulk have a wide range of applications in string theory. They are used to study the properties of the bulk space, to understand the behavior of strings in the bulk, and to investigate the bulk-boundary correspondence. In this section, we will explore some of these applications in more detail.

##### Probing the Bulk Space

Pointlike probes of the bulk are used to probe the bulk space and measure its properties. These probes are typically represented by point-like objects, such as quantum dots, which are used to interact with the bulk space and measure its properties. The potential at the probe, denoted as $\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is described by a boundary value problem of electrostatics. The boundary $\mathcal{S}$ is given by the surfaces of the sample and the probe, assumed to be connected at infinity. $$
\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})=\iiint\limits_\mathcal{V} G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') \frac{\rho(\mathbf{r}')}{e}d^3\mathbf{r}'+ \frac{\epsilon_0}{e}\oint\limits_\mathcal{S} \bigg[G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial\Phi(\mathbf{r}')}{\partial \mathbf{n}'}-\Phi(\mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') }{\partial \mathbf{n}'}\bigg]d^2\mathbf{r}'.
$$

In this expression, $\Phi_\text{QD}$ depends on the charge density $\rho$ inside $\mathcal{V}$ and on the potential $\Phi$ on $\mathcal{S}$ weighted by the Green's function $G(\mathbf{r},\mathbf{r}')$. The Green's function is given by

$$

$$

where $F$ satisfies the Laplace equation. By specifying $F$ and thus defining the boundary conditions, these equations can be used to obtain the relation between $\Phi_\text{QD}$ and the surface potential $\Phi_\text{s}(\mathbf{r}')$, which is the potential at the surface of the sample.

##### Studying the Behavior of Strings in the Bulk

Pointlike probes of the bulk are also used to study the behavior of strings in the bulk. The interaction between a string and a pointlike probe can be described by the same boundary value problem of electrostatics as the interaction between the probe and the bulk space. By studying these interactions, we can gain insight into the behavior of strings in the bulk and the rules governing their interactions with the bulk space.

##### Investigating the Bulk-Boundary Correspondence

Finally, pointlike probes of the bulk are used to investigate the bulk-boundary correspondence. This correspondence describes the relationship between the properties of the bulk space and the boundary conditions imposed on it. By studying the interaction between pointlike probes and the bulk space, we can gain insight into this correspondence and understand how the properties of the bulk space are determined by the boundary conditions.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of pointlike probes of the bulk and explore their applications in more detail.




#### 8.1c Advanced Topics on Pointlike Probes of the Bulk

In the previous sections, we have discussed the role of pointlike probes in string theory and their use in probing the bulk space. In this section, we will delve deeper into the advanced topics related to pointlike probes of the bulk.

#### 8.1c.1 Pointlike Probes and the Bulk-Boundary Correspondence

The bulk-boundary correspondence is a fundamental concept in string theory, and pointlike probes play a crucial role in understanding this correspondence. The bulk-boundary correspondence describes the relationship between the properties of the bulk space and the boundary conditions imposed on it. 

Pointlike probes, such as quantum dots, are used to probe the bulk space and measure its properties. The potential at the probe, denoted as $\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is described by a boundary value problem of electrostatics. The boundary $\mathcal{S}$ is given by the surfaces of the sample and the probe, assumed to be connected at infinity. The potential $\Phi_\text{QD}$ can be expressed using the Green's function formalism as a sum over volume and surface integrals, where $\mathcal{V}$ denotes the volume enclosed by $\mathcal{S}$ and $\mathbf{n}'$ is the surface normal.

$$
\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})=\iiint\limits_\mathcal{V} G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') \frac{\rho(\mathbf{r}')}{e}d^3\mathbf{r}'+ \frac{\epsilon_0}{e}\oint\limits_\mathcal{S} \bigg[G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial\Phi(\mathbf{r}')}{\partial \mathbf{n}'}-\Phi(\mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') }{\partial \mathbf{n}'}\bigg]d^2\mathbf{r}'.
$$

In this expression, $\Phi_\text{QD}$ depends on the charge density $\rho$ inside the volume $\mathcal{V}$ and on the surface $\mathcal{S}$. The bulk-boundary correspondence is then used to relate the properties of the bulk space, such as the charge density $\rho$, to the boundary conditions imposed on the bulk space.

#### 8.1c.2 Pointlike Probes and the Proton Radius Puzzle

The proton radius puzzle is another advanced topic related to pointlike probes of the bulk. The proton radius puzzle refers to the discrepancy between the measured proton radius and the predicted proton radius based on the Standard Model of particle physics.

Pointlike probes, such as quantum dots, have been used to probe the proton radius. The potential at the probe, denoted as $\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is described by a boundary value problem of electrostatics, similar to the bulk-boundary correspondence. The potential $\Phi_\text{QD}$ can be expressed using the Green's function formalism as a sum over volume and surface integrals, where $\mathcal{V}$ denotes the volume enclosed by $\mathcal{S}$ and $\mathbf{n}'$ is the surface normal.

$$
\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})=\iiint\limits_\mathcal{V} G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') \frac{\rho(\mathbf{r}')}{e}d^3\mathbf{r}'+ \frac{\epsilon_0}{e}\oint\limits_\mathcal{S} \bigg[G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial\Phi(\mathbf{r}')}{\partial \mathbf{n}'}-\Phi(\mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') }{\partial \mathbf{n}'}\bigg]d^2\mathbf{r}'.
$$

In this expression, $\Phi_\text{QD}$ depends on the charge density $\rho$ inside the volume $\mathcal{V}$ and on the surface $\mathcal{S}$. The proton radius puzzle is then used to relate the properties of the proton, such as the charge density $\rho$, to the boundary conditions imposed on the proton.

#### 8.1c.3 Pointlike Probes and the Quasi-Free Hypothesis

The quasi-free hypothesis is another advanced topic related to pointlike probes of the bulk. The quasi-free hypothesis refers to the assumption that the proton can be treated as a quasi-free particle, similar to the quasi-free electron in quantum mechanics.

Pointlike probes, such as quantum dots, have been used to probe the quasi-free hypothesis. The potential at the probe, denoted as $\Phi_\text{QD} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is described by a boundary value problem of electrostatics, similar to the bulk-boundary correspondence and the proton radius puzzle. The potential $\Phi_\text{QD}$ can be expressed using the Green's function formalism as a sum over volume and surface integrals, where $\mathcal{V}$ denotes the volume enclosed by $\mathcal{S}$ and $\mathbf{n}'$ is the surface normal.

$$

#### 8.2a Definition and Characteristics of Baryons and Branes in AdS

In the previous sections, we have discussed the role of pointlike probes in string theory and their use in probing the bulk space. In this section, we will explore the concept of baryons and branes in AdS space and their characteristics.

#### 8.2a.1 Baryons in AdS

Baryons are particles that are composed of three quarks. In the context of string theory, baryons are often studied in AdS space, a type of anti-de Sitter space. AdS space is a solution to the equations of motion for the theory of gravitation with Einstein–Hilbert action with negative cosmological constant $\Lambda$, where $\Lambda < 0$. 

The metric of AdS space in global coordinates is given by:

$$
ds^2 = \alpha^2\left(-\cosh^2\rho \, d\tau^2 + \, d\rho^2 + \sinh^2\rho \, d\Omega_{n-2}^2\right)
$$

where $\alpha$ is the radius of AdS space, $\rho$ is the radial coordinate, $\tau$ is the time coordinate, and $d\Omega_{n-2}^2$ is the metric on the $(n-2)$-dimensional unit sphere.

Baryons in AdS space are often studied using the AdS/CFT correspondence, which relates the properties of the bulk space to the boundary conditions imposed on it. The AdS/CFT correspondence is a powerful tool for understanding the properties of baryons in AdS space.

#### 8.2a.2 Branes in AdS

Branes, or boundary objects, are another important concept in string theory. In AdS space, branes can be used to study the properties of the bulk space. The bulk-boundary correspondence, which describes the relationship between the properties of the bulk space and the boundary conditions imposed on it, is often used in conjunction with branes in AdS space.

The potential at the brane, denoted as $\Phi_\text{Brane} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is described by a boundary value problem of electrostatics. The boundary $\mathcal{S}$ is given by the surfaces of the sample and the brane, assumed to be connected at infinity. The potential $\Phi_\text{Brane}$ can be expressed using the Green's function formalism as a sum over volume and surface integrals, where $\mathcal{V}$ denotes the volume enclosed by $\mathcal{S}$ and $\mathbf{n}'$ is the surface normal.

$$
\Phi_\text{Brane} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})=\iiint\limits_\mathcal{V} G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') \frac{\rho(\mathbf{r}')}{e}d^3\mathbf{r}'+ \frac{\epsilon_0}{e}\oint\limits_\mathcal{S} \bigg[G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial\Phi(\mathbf{r}')}{\partial \mathbf{n}'}-\Phi(\mathbf{r}')\frac{\partial G(\mathbf{r}, \mathbf{r}') }{\partial \mathbf{n}'}\bigg]d^2\mathbf{r}'.
$$

In this expression, $\Phi_\text{Brane}$ depends on the charge density $\rho$ inside the volume $\mathcal{V}$ and on the surface $\mathcal{S}$. The bulk-boundary correspondence is then used to relate the properties of the bulk space, such as the charge density $\rho$, to the boundary conditions imposed on the bulk space.

#### 8.2a.3 Baryons and Branes in AdS

Baryons and branes in AdS space are often studied together, as they provide a powerful tool for understanding the properties of the bulk space. The AdS/CFT correspondence, the bulk-boundary correspondence, and the concept of the proton radius $P$ are all important tools in this study.

The proton radius $P$ is a key parameter in the study of baryons and branes in AdS space. It is defined as the radius of the proton, which is the smallest baryon. The proton radius $P$ is related to the charge density $\rho$ inside the proton, and can be calculated using the AdS/CFT correspondence and the bulk-boundary correspondence.

In the context of baryons, the AdS/CFT correspondence allows us to study the properties of baryons in AdS space by considering the boundary conditions imposed on the AdS space. This approach has been used to study various properties of baryons, including their masses, decay rates, and interactions with other particles.

#### 8.2b.2 Branes in AdS and the Bulk-Boundary Correspondence

Branes in AdS space play a crucial role in the bulk-boundary correspondence. The bulk-boundary correspondence describes the relationship between the properties of the bulk space and the boundary conditions imposed on it. 

In the context of branes, the bulk-boundary correspondence allows us to study the properties of the bulk space by considering the boundary conditions imposed on the brane. This approach has been used to study various properties of the bulk space, including its geometry, its interactions with other objects in the bulk, and its response to perturbations.

The holographic principle, a key concept in string theory, also plays a crucial role in the study of baryons and branes in AdS space. The holographic principle suggests that all the information about the bulk space can be encoded in the boundary conditions imposed on it.

In the context of baryons and branes, the holographic principle allows us to study the properties of the bulk space by considering the boundary conditions imposed on it. #### 8.2c Baryons and Branes in AdS in String Theory

#### 8.2c.1 Baryons in AdS and String Theory

In string theory, baryons are often studied in the context of AdS space. The AdS/CFT correspondence allows us to study the properties of baryons in AdS space by considering the boundary conditions imposed on the AdS space. 

In string theory, baryons are often represented as D-branes. A D-brane is a higher-dimensional object that can be thought of as a generalization of a string. The world-volume of a D-brane is a (p+1)-dimensional submanifold of spacetime, where p is the dimension of the brane. 

The potential at the brane, denoted as $\Phi_\text{Brane} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is described by a boundary value problem of electrostatics. The boundary $\mathcal{S}$ is given by the surfaces of the sample and the brane, assumed to be connected at infinity. The potential $\Phi_\text{Brane}$ can be expressed using the Coulomb law as:

$$
\Phi_\text{Brane} = \frac{1}{4\pi\varepsilon_0} \frac{Q_\text{Brane}}{r}
$$

where $Q_\text{Brane}$ is the charge of the brane, and $r$ is the distance from the brane.

#### 8.2c.2 Branes in AdS and String Theory

Branes in AdS space play a crucial role in the bulk-boundary correspondence. The bulk-boundary correspondence describes the relationship between the properties of the bulk space and the boundary conditions imposed on it. 

In string theory, branes are often represented as D-branes. The world-volume of a D-brane is a (p+1)-dimensional submanifold of spacetime, where p is the dimension of the brane. The boundary of the world-volume is the brane itself.

The potential at the brane, denoted as $\Phi_\text{Brane} = \Phi(\mathbf{r})$, is described by a boundary value problem of electrostatics. The boundary $\mathcal{S}$ is given by the surfaces of the sample and the brane, assumed to be connected at infinity. The potential $\Phi_\text{Brane}$ can be expressed using the Coulomb law as:

$$
\Phi_\text{Brane} = \frac{1}{4\pi\varepsilon_0} \frac{Q_\text{Brane}}{r}
$$

where $Q_\text{Brane}$ is the charge of the brane, and $r$ is the distance from the brane.

#### 8.2c.3 Baryons and Branes in AdS and the Holographic Principle

#### 8.2c Advanced Topics on Baryons and Branes in AdS

In this section, we will explore some advanced topics related to baryons and branes in AdS space. These topics will provide a deeper understanding of the role of these objects in string theory and their implications for the AdS/CFT correspondence.

#### 8.2c.1 Baryons and the AdS/CFT Correspondence

The AdS/CFT correspondence has been instrumental in the study of baryons in AdS space. However, there are still many aspects of this correspondence that are not fully understood. For instance, the correspondence is typically formulated for large 't Hooft coupling, where the AdS space is approximated as a semi-classical geometry. It is not clear how to extend this correspondence to the regime of small 't Hooft coupling, where the AdS space is expected to deviate significantly from a semi-classical geometry.

Moreover, the correspondence is typically formulated for conformal field theories (CFTs) on the boundary of AdS space. It is not clear how to extend this correspondence to non-conformal field theories, which are more relevant for many physical applications.

#### 8.2c.2 Branes and the Bulk-Boundary Correspondence

The bulk-boundary correspondence is a powerful tool for studying the properties of the bulk space by considering the boundary conditions imposed on it. However, there are still many aspects of this correspondence that are not fully understood. For instance, the correspondence is typically formulated for branes that are localized in the bulk space. It is not clear how to extend this correspondence to branes that are delocalized in the bulk space, which are expected to play a crucial role in certain physical applications.

Moreover, the correspondence is typically formulated for branes that are static in the bulk space. It is not clear how to extend this correspondence to branes that are dynamical in the bulk space, which are expected to play a crucial role in certain physical applications.

#### 8.2c.3 Baryons and Branes in AdS and the Holographic Principle

The holographic principle, which suggests that all the information about the bulk space can be encoded in the boundary conditions imposed on it, is a key concept in string theory. However, there are still many aspects of this principle that are not fully understood. For instance, the principle is typically formulated for AdS space, which is a vacuum solution of the Einstein equations. It is not clear how to extend this principle to more general solutions of the Einstein equations, which are expected to describe more realistic physical systems.

Moreover, the principle is typically formulated for the case of AdS space with a negative cosmological constant. It is not clear how to extend this principle to the case of AdS space with a positive cosmological constant, which is expected to describe certain physical systems.




#### 8.3a Definition and Characteristics of Non-Spherical Horizons

Non-spherical horizons (NSHs) are a generalization of the concept of a horizon in general relativity. They are defined as the boundary of a region in spacetime where the outgoing expansion rate along the null normal to the boundary is vanishing. This definition is expressed in the language of the null formalism (NP) with the convention $\{(-,+,+,+); l^an_a=-1, m^a\bar{m}_a=1\}$, where $l^a$ is the null normal to the boundary, and $m^a$ is a null vector tangent to the boundary.

The outgoing expansion rate along $l^a$ is denoted by $\theta_{(l)}$, and it is defined as $\theta_{(l)} = l^a_{;a}$, where $l^a_{;a}$ is the covariant derivative of $l^a$. According to the Raychaudhuri-NP "expansion-twist" equation, the outgoing expansion rate is related to the NP-shear coefficient $\sigma$ as follows:

$$
\theta_{(l)} = -2\sigma.
$$

This equation implies that the product $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$ is nonnegative, and consequently, $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$ and $R_{ab}l^a l^b$ must be simultaneously zero on the boundary. This result is a direct consequence of the energy condition (iii) in the definition of NSHs, which states that the causal vector $-T^a_b l^b$ in condition (iii) is proportional to $l^a$.

The vanishing of the outgoing expansion rate along $l^a$ on the boundary implies that the boundary is nonevolutional, and all foliation leaves $S^2$ look identical with one another. This property is crucial for the definition of NSHs, as it ensures that the boundary is a stable and well-defined object in spacetime.

In the next section, we will explore the implications of these characteristics for the study of NSHs in various physical systems.

#### 8.3b Properties of Non-Spherical Horizons

The properties of non-spherical horizons (NSHs) are largely determined by their definition and the characteristics discussed in the previous section. These properties are crucial for understanding the behavior of NSHs in various physical systems.

1. **Nonevolutionality**: As mentioned earlier, the boundary of an NSH is nonevolutional. This means that the boundary does not change with time, and all foliation leaves $S^2$ look identical with one another. This property is a direct consequence of the vanishing of the outgoing expansion rate along the null normal to the boundary.

2. **Stability**: The stability of an NSH is determined by the product $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$, which is nonnegative according to the Raychaudhuri-NP "expansion-twist" equation. This implies that the boundary is stable and well-defined in spacetime.

3. **Relation to the Energy Condition**: The energy condition (iii) in the definition of NSHs states that the causal vector $-T^a_b l^b$ in condition (iii) is proportional to $l^a$. This condition is crucial for the vanishing of the outgoing expansion rate along $l^a$ on the boundary.

4. **Relation to the NP-Shear Coefficient**: The NP-shear coefficient $\sigma$ is related to the outgoing expansion rate along $l^a$ as follows: $\theta_{(l)} = -2\sigma$. This equation implies that the product $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$ is nonnegative, and consequently, $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$ and $R_{ab}l^a l^b$ must be simultaneously zero on the boundary.

5. **Relation to the Ricci Tensor**: The product $R_{ab}l^a l^b$ is zero on the boundary according to the energy condition (iii). This implies that the Ricci tensor $R_{ab}$ is orthogonal to the boundary.

These properties of NSHs provide a deeper understanding of their behavior in various physical systems. In the next section, we will explore the implications of these properties for the study of NSHs in string theory.

#### 8.3c Non-Spherical Horizons in String Theory

In the context of string theory, non-spherical horizons (NSHs) play a crucial role in the study of black holes and other gravitational phenomena. The properties of NSHs in string theory are largely determined by the characteristics discussed in the previous section, but they also exhibit some unique features that are specific to the string theory context.

1. **Stringy Corrections**: In string theory, the classical description of gravity is modified by quantum effects. These quantum effects can lead to deviations from the classical predictions, known as stringy corrections. These corrections can affect the properties of NSHs, leading to a more complex and interesting behavior.

2. **Holographic Duality**: The AdS/CFT correspondence, or holographic duality, is a fundamental concept in string theory. It suggests that certain gravitational theories in higher dimensions can be described by field theories in lower dimensions. This duality can be applied to NSHs, leading to new insights into their properties and behavior.

3. **Entropy of Black Holes**: In string theory, the entropy of black holes is related to the area of the horizon. For NSHs, this relation can be extended to non-spherical black holes, leading to a more general understanding of black hole entropy.

4. **Hawking Radiation**: In string theory, Hawking radiation is interpreted as the emission of strings. This interpretation can be applied to NSHs, leading to a more detailed understanding of the Hawking radiation process.

5. **Cosmic Censorship Conjecture**: The cosmic censorship conjecture, which states that naked singularities do not exist, can be applied to NSHs. This conjecture can provide insights into the behavior of NSHs near the singularity.

These features of NSHs in string theory provide a rich and fascinating area of study. In the next section, we will explore some specific examples of NSHs in string theory, including the BTZ black hole and the Kerr black hole.

#### 8.3c Advanced Topics on Non-Spherical Horizons

In the previous sections, we have discussed the basic properties of non-spherical horizons (NSHs) and their role in string theory. In this section, we will delve deeper into some advanced topics related to NSHs.

#### 8.3c.1 Non-Spherical Horizons and the Null Energy Condition

The null energy condition (NEC) is a fundamental concept in general relativity that provides a constraint on the energy-momentum tensor of a system. It states that the energy density of a system must be non-negative when evaluated in a null frame. In the context of NSHs, the NEC plays a crucial role in determining the properties of the horizon.

The NEC can be expressed in the language of the NP formalism as follows:

$$
R_{ab}l^a l^b \geq 0,
$$

where $R_{ab}$ is the Ricci tensor, and $l^a$ is the null normal to the horizon. This condition implies that the product $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$ is nonnegative, and consequently, $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$ and $R_{ab}l^a l^b$ must be simultaneously zero on the horizon. This result is a direct consequence of the energy condition (iii) in the definition of NSHs, which states that the causal vector $-T^a_b l^b$ in condition (iii) is proportional to $l^a$.

#### 8.3c.2 Non-Spherical Horizons and the Raychaudhuri-NP Equation

The Raychaudhuri-NP equation is a fundamental equation in the study of NSHs. It relates the outgoing expansion rate along the null normal to the horizon to the NP-shear coefficient $\sigma$. The equation is given by:

$$
\theta_{(l)} = -2\sigma.
$$

This equation implies that the product $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$ is nonnegative, and consequently, $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$ and $R_{ab}l^a l^b$ must be simultaneously zero on the horizon. This result is a direct consequence of the energy condition (iii) in the definition of NSHs, which states that the causal vector $-T^a_b l^b$ in condition (iii) is proportional to $l^a$.

#### 8.3c.3 Non-Spherical Horizons and the Quasilocal Definition of Black Holes

The quasilocal definition of black holes is a powerful tool in the study of NSHs. It provides a way to define a black hole in terms of the properties of the horizon, without reference to the interior of the black hole. This definition is particularly useful in the study of NSHs, as it allows us to focus on the properties of the horizon without having to consider the complex dynamics of the interior of the black hole.

The quasilocal definition of black holes is based on the concept of a trapped surface, which is a region of spacetime where the outgoing expansion rate along the null normal to the surface is negative. In the context of NSHs, the trapped surface is replaced by the non-expanding horizon (NEH), which is a region of spacetime where the outgoing expansion rate along the null normal to the horizon is zero.

The quasilocal definition of black holes can be expressed in the language of the NP formalism as follows:

$$
\theta_{(l)} = -2\sigma.
$$

This equation implies that the product $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$ is nonnegative, and consequently, $\sigma\bar{\sigma}$ and $R_{ab}l^a l^b$ must be simultaneously zero on the horizon. This result is a direct consequence of the energy condition (iii) in the definition of NSHs, which states that the causal vector $-T^a_b l^b$ in condition (iii) is proportional to $l^a$.

### Conclusion

In this chapter, we have explored the concept of probes in the context of string theory. We have seen how these probes can be used to probe the bulk of the theory, providing us with valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of the theory. We have also seen how these probes can be used to study the behavior of the theory at different points in space and time, allowing us to gain a deeper understanding of the theory.

We have also discussed the role of probes in the context of the AdS/CFT correspondence. We have seen how these probes can be used to study the behavior of the theory on the boundary of the AdS space, providing us with valuable insights into the behavior of the theory on the boundary of the AdS space.

Overall, the study of probes in string theory has proven to be a powerful tool in our exploration of the theory. It has allowed us to gain a deeper understanding of the theory, providing us with valuable insights into the structure and dynamics of the theory. As we continue to explore the theory, we can expect to see even more applications of probes, further enhancing our understanding of the theory.

## Chapter: Non-Spherical Horizons

### Introduction

In the previous chapters, we have explored the fundamental concepts of string theory, including the nature of strings, their interactions, and the role of space-time in these interactions. We have also delved into the concept of black holes and their significance in string theory. In this chapter, we will further expand our understanding of black holes by exploring non-spherical horizons.

Non-spherical horizons are a fascinating aspect of black holes in string theory. They represent a departure from the traditional spherical black holes, and their study has led to significant insights into the nature of space-time and the behavior of strings within it. The study of non-spherical horizons is a rapidly evolving field, with new developments and theories being proposed regularly.

In this chapter, we will begin by introducing the concept of non-spherical horizons and discussing their properties. We will then delve into the mathematical formalism used to describe these horizons, including the use of differential equations and tensor calculus. We will also explore the physical implications of these mathematical descriptions, including the behavior of strings within non-spherical horizons.

We will also discuss the role of non-spherical horizons in the context of the AdS/CFT correspondence, a fundamental concept in string theory. This correspondence suggests a deep connection between the behavior of strings in a higher-dimensional space-time and the behavior of particles in a lower-dimensional conformal field theory. Non-spherical horizons play a crucial role in this correspondence, and their study can provide valuable insights into the nature of this correspondence.

Finally, we will discuss some of the current challenges and open questions in the study of non-spherical horizons. These include the development of a fully consistent theory of non-spherical horizons, the understanding of the physical implications of these horizons, and the exploration of their role in the broader context of string theory.

In this chapter, we will use the popular Markdown format to present our content, allowing for a clear and concise presentation of the material. We will also use the MathJax library to render mathematical expressions and equations, ensuring a high level of precision and clarity in our discussions.




#### 8.4a Overview of Other Examples of the Correspondence

In the previous sections, we have discussed the AdS/CFT correspondence and its applications in various contexts. In this section, we will explore some other examples of the correspondence, including the D3-D7 system, the D3-D5 system, and the D3-D9 system.

#### 8.4a.1 The D3-D7 System

The D3-D7 system is a specific example of the AdS/CFT correspondence. It involves a D3-brane and a D7-brane in type IIB string theory. The D3-brane is located at the tip of the cone, while the D7-brane is located at the vertex of the cone. The correspondence between the two systems is given by the map:

$$
\mathbb{R}^{1,3} \times S^5 \cong \mathbb{R}^{1,3} \times \mathbb{C}^4/\mathbb{Z}_k,
$$

where $\mathbb{R}^{1,3}$ is the Minkowski space, $S^5$ is the five-sphere, and $\mathbb{C}^4/\mathbb{Z}_k$ is the cone. This map preserves the metric and the flux of the system, and it also preserves the supersymmetry.

The D3-D7 system has been studied extensively in the literature, and it has been shown to exhibit interesting properties, such as the existence of a non-trivial supergravity solution and the presence of a non-trivial flux. These properties make the D3-D7 system a useful tool for studying the AdS/CFT correspondence.

#### 8.4a.2 The D3-D5 System

The D3-D5 system is another specific example of the AdS/CFT correspondence. It involves a D3-brane and a D5-brane in type IIB string theory. The D3-brane is located at the tip of the cone, while the D5-brane is located at the vertex of the cone. The correspondence between the two systems is given by the map:

$$
\mathbb{R}^{1,3} \times S^5 \cong \mathbb{R}^{1,3} \times \mathbb{C}^4/\mathbb{Z}_k,
$$

where $\mathbb{R}^{1,3}$ is the Minkowski space, $S^5$ is the five-sphere, and $\mathbb{C}^4/\mathbb{Z}_k$ is the cone. This map preserves the metric and the flux of the system, and it also preserves the supersymmetry.

The D3-D5 system has been studied extensively in the literature, and it has been shown to exhibit interesting properties, such as the existence of a non-trivial supergravity solution and the presence of a non-trivial flux. These properties make the D3-D5 system a useful tool for studying the AdS/CFT correspondence.

#### 8.4a.3 The D3-D9 System

The D3-D9 system is a specific example of the AdS/CFT correspondence. It involves a D3-brane and a D9-brane in type IIB string theory. The D3-brane is located at the tip of the cone, while the D9-brane is located at the vertex of the cone. The correspondence between the two systems is given by the map:

#### 8.4a.4 Other Examples of the Correspondence

In addition to the D3-D7, D3-D5, and D3-D9 systems, there are many other examples of the AdS/CFT correspondence. These include the D1-D9 system, the D1-D5 system, and the D1-D3 system. Each of these systems has its own unique properties and applications, and they all contribute to our understanding of the AdS/CFT correspondence.

#### 8.4b.3 The D3-D9 System

\mathbb{R}^{1,3} \times S^5 \cong \mathbb{R}^{1,3} \times \mathbb{C}^4/\mathbb{Z}_k,
$$

where $\mathbb{R}^{1,3}$ is the Minkowski space, $S^5$ is the five-sphere, and $\mathbb{C}^4/\mathbb{Z}_k$ is the cone. This map preserves the metric and the flux of the system, and it also preserves the supersymmetry.

The D3-D9 system has been studied extensively in the literature, and it has been shown to exhibit interesting properties, such as the existence of a non-trivial supergravity solution and the presence of a non-trivial flux. These properties make the D3-D9 system a useful tool for studying the AdS/CFT correspondence.




#### 8.4c Advanced Topics on Other Examples of the Correspondence

In the previous sections, we have discussed the D3-D7 and D3-D5 systems, which are specific examples of the AdS/CFT correspondence. In this section, we will explore some advanced topics related to these systems and their implications for the AdS/CFT correspondence.

#### 8.4c.1 The D3-D7 System and the AdS/CFT Correspondence

The D3-D7 system is a powerful tool for studying the AdS/CFT correspondence. It allows us to map the bulk theory, described by the D3-brane, to the boundary theory, described by the D7-brane. This mapping preserves the metric, flux, and supersymmetry of the system, making it a useful tool for studying the correspondence.

One of the key properties of the D3-D7 system is the existence of a non-trivial supergravity solution. This solution describes the interaction between the D3-brane and the D7-brane, and it is crucial for understanding the dynamics of the system. The existence of this solution has been shown to be related to the presence of a non-trivial flux in the system.

#### 8.4c.2 The D3-D5 System and the AdS/CFT Correspondence

The D3-D5 system is another important example of the AdS/CFT correspondence. It involves a D3-brane and a D5-brane, and the correspondence between the two systems is given by the map:

$$
\mathbb{R}^{1,3} \times S^5 \cong \mathbb{R}^{1,3} \times \mathbb{C}^4/\mathbb{Z}_k,
$$

where $\mathbb{R}^{1,3}$ is the Minkowski space, $S^5$ is the five-sphere, and $\mathbb{C}^4/\mathbb{Z}_k$ is the cone. This map preserves the metric and the flux of the system, and it also preserves the supersymmetry.

The D3-D5 system has been studied extensively in the literature, and it has been shown to exhibit interesting properties, such as the existence of a non-trivial supergravity solution and the presence of a non-trivial flux. These properties make the D3-D5 system a useful tool for studying the AdS/CFT correspondence.

#### 8.4c.3 The AdS/CFT Correspondence and the Bulk-Boundary Correspondence

The AdS/CFT correspondence is a powerful tool for studying the dynamics of the bulk theory. It allows us to map the bulk theory to the boundary theory, and vice versa. This mapping is known as the bulk-boundary correspondence.

The bulk-boundary correspondence is a crucial aspect of the AdS/CFT correspondence. It allows us to study the dynamics of the bulk theory by studying the boundary theory. This is particularly useful in systems where the bulk theory is difficult to analyze directly, such as in the D3-D7 and D3-D5 systems.

In conclusion, the AdS/CFT correspondence is a powerful tool for studying the dynamics of the bulk theory. The D3-D7 and D3-D5 systems are important examples of this correspondence, and they have been studied extensively in the literature. The bulk-boundary correspondence is a crucial aspect of the AdS/CFT correspondence, and it allows us to study the dynamics of the bulk theory by studying the boundary theory.

We have seen how these probes can be used to study the properties of the bulk and how they can provide valuable insights into the behavior of the theory. We have also discussed the different types of probes that can be used, such as D-branes and fundamental strings, and how they can be used to probe different regions of the bulk.

One of the key takeaways from this chapter is the importance of understanding the bulk in string theory. By studying the bulk, we can gain a deeper understanding of the theory and its underlying principles. Probes of the bulk allow us to access the bulk and study its properties, providing us with a more complete understanding of the theory.

Another important aspect of this chapter is the concept of duality. We have seen how the bulk can be described in terms of different dual descriptions, each providing a different perspective on the theory. This duality allows us to explore the bulk from different angles and gain a more comprehensive understanding of the theory.

In conclusion, probes of the bulk play a crucial role in string theory. They allow us to study the bulk and gain a deeper understanding of the theory. By exploring the bulk through different probes and dual descriptions, we can gain a more complete understanding of string theory and its underlying principles.

The string swampland is a region in the parameter space of string theory that is not well-understood. It is a region where the traditional methods of string theory, such as perturbation theory, fail to provide a complete description of the theory. This is due to the fact that the string swampland is filled with a vast number of vacua, or possible configurations of the theory, that are difficult to analyze using traditional methods.

In this chapter, we will explore the string swampland and its implications for string theory. We will discuss the various approaches that have been taken to understand this region, including the use of non-perturbative techniques and the concept of string duality. ### Introduction

In this chapter, we will delve into the fascinating world of quantum gravity and black hole thermodynamics. It seeks to explain the behavior of the universe at its most fundamental level, where quantum effects and gravitational effects cannot be ignored.

On the other hand, black hole thermodynamics is a branch of physics that studies the thermodynamic properties of black holes. It is a field that has seen significant advancements in recent years, particularly with the discovery of Hawking radiation and the concept of black hole entropy. These developments have led to a deeper understanding of the nature of black holes and their role in the universe.

In this chapter, we will explore the fundamental principles of quantum gravity and black hole thermodynamics, and how they are interconnected. ### Subsection: 9.1a Definition and Characteristics of Confinement and Quantum Gravity

Confinement is a fundamental concept in quantum mechanics that describes the behavior of particles within a confined space. In the context of quantum gravity, confinement plays a crucial role in understanding the behavior of particles at the Planck scale, where quantum effects and gravitational effects cannot be ignored.

#### 9.1a.1 Definition of Confinement

Confinement can be defined as the phenomenon where particles are confined within a finite region of space due to the presence of a potential barrier. This barrier can be created by various means, such as a physical barrier, a potential energy barrier, or a combination of both. The confinement of particles leads to the formation of a potential well, within which the particles are trapped and their behavior is governed by the principles of quantum mechanics.

In the context of quantum gravity, confinement is particularly important as it allows us to understand the behavior of particles at the Planck scale. At this scale, the effects of quantum mechanics and general relativity become indistinguishable, and the concept of confinement becomes crucial in describing the behavior of particles.

#### 9.1a.2 Characteristics of Confinement

Confinement has several key characteristics that are important to understand in the context of quantum gravity. These include:

1. **Quantum Confinement:** At the Planck scale, the effects of quantum mechanics and general relativity become indistinguishable. This leads to the phenomenon of quantum confinement, where particles are confined within a finite region of space due to the effects of quantum mechanics.

2. **Gravitational Confinement:** In addition to quantum confinement, particles at the Planck scale also experience gravitational confinement due to the effects of general relativity. This leads to the formation of a potential well, within which the particles are trapped.

3. **Quantum Tunneling:** Due to the wave-like nature of particles at the Planck scale, particles can exhibit quantum tunneling behavior, allowing them to escape from the potential well created by confinement. This phenomenon is crucial in understanding the behavior of particles at the Planck scale.

In the next section, we will delve deeper into the concept of confinement and its implications for quantum gravity and black hole thermodynamics.




### Subsection: 9.1b Role of Confinement and Quantum Gravity in String Theory

Confinement plays a crucial role in string theory, a theoretical framework that attempts to unify all fundamental interactions of nature. In string theory, particles are represented as one-dimensional objects called strings, and the concept of confinement is used to explain the behavior of these strings at the Planck scale.

#### 9.1b.1 String Confinement

In string theory, confinement is used to explain the behavior of strings at the Planck scale. At this scale, the effects of quantum mechanics and general relativity become indistinguishable, and the concept of confinement becomes crucial in describing the behavior of strings.

The confinement of strings leads to the formation of a potential well, within which the strings are trapped and their behavior is governed by the principles of quantum mechanics. This allows us to understand the behavior of strings at the Planck scale, where the effects of quantum mechanics and general relativity become indistinguishable.

#### 9.1b.2 Quantum Gravity and String Theory

Quantum gravity is a fundamental theory that combines quantum mechanics and general relativity. It is a theory that attempts to describe the behavior of the universe at the most fundamental level. In string theory, quantum gravity is used to explain the behavior of strings at the Planck scale.

The concept of confinement is particularly important in string theory, as it allows us to understand the behavior of strings at the Planck scale. At this scale, the effects of quantum mechanics and general relativity become indistinguishable, and the concept of confinement becomes crucial in describing the behavior of strings.

In conclusion, confinement plays a crucial role in string theory, allowing us to understand the behavior of strings at the Planck scale. It is a fundamental concept that is used to explain the behavior of particles and strings at the most fundamental level.




### Subsection: 9.1c Advanced Topics on Confinement and Quantum Gravity

In the previous sections, we have discussed the concept of confinement and its role in string theory. In this section, we will delve deeper into advanced topics related to confinement and quantum gravity.

#### 9.1c.1 Confinement and the Quantum Master Constraint

The quantum master constraint is a fundamental concept in loop quantum gravity, a theory that attempts to reconcile quantum mechanics and general relativity. It is a mathematical expression that encapsulates the principles of quantum mechanics and general relativity, and it plays a crucial role in understanding the behavior of strings at the Planck scale.

The quantum master constraint can be defined as follows:

$$
\hat{C} = \int \left(\frac{\delta S}{\delta N}\right)^2 d^3x
$$

where $\hat{C}$ is the quantum master constraint operator, $S$ is the action, $N$ is the lapse function, and the integral is taken over all space. This equation represents the quantum version of the classical master constraint, which is a key equation in classical mechanics.

The quantum master constraint is a powerful tool in loop quantum gravity, as it allows us to express the principles of quantum mechanics and general relativity in a mathematically consistent way. It also plays a crucial role in understanding the behavior of strings at the Planck scale, where the effects of quantum mechanics and general relativity become indistinguishable.

#### 9.1c.2 Confinement and the Quantum Potential

The quantum potential is another important concept in loop quantum gravity. It is a mathematical expression that encapsulates the effects of quantum mechanics on the behavior of strings at the Planck scale.

The quantum potential $Q$ is defined as follows:

$$
Q = \frac{\hbar}{2mi}\left(\frac{\delta}{\delta x}\right)^2
$$

where $\hbar$ is the reduced Planck's constant, $m$ is the mass of the string, $i$ is the imaginary unit, and $\delta/\delta x$ is the functional derivative with respect to position.

The quantum potential plays a crucial role in understanding the behavior of strings at the Planck scale, where the effects of quantum mechanics become dominant. It also plays a key role in the quantum master constraint, as it is one of the terms that contribute to the quantum master constraint operator.

#### 9.1c.3 Confinement and the Quantum Potential Barrier

The quantum potential barrier is a concept that arises from the quantum potential. It is a mathematical expression that represents the barrier that confines strings at the Planck scale.

The quantum potential barrier $V_Q$ is defined as follows:

$$
V_Q = \frac{\hbar}{2mi}\left(\frac{\delta}{\delta x}\right)^2
$$

where the symbols have the same meaning as in the definition of the quantum potential.

The quantum potential barrier plays a crucial role in understanding the behavior of strings at the Planck scale. It represents the barrier that confines strings and prevents them from escaping into the classical regime. This barrier is a direct consequence of the quantum nature of strings and is a key aspect of quantum gravity.

In the next section, we will discuss the concept of black hole thermodynamics and its role in quantum gravity.
